  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_3887c2a8-93fb-45bb-a02c-bd2849391075'
      });
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer2684316768460963298">Todd Anderson and his friends at Welton Academy can hardly believe how different life is since their new English professor, the flamboyant John Keating, has challenged them to "make your lives extraordinary! " Inspired by Keating, the boys resurrect the Dead Poets Society--a secret club where, free from the constraints and expectations of school and parents, they let their</span>
  <span id="freeText2684316768460963298" style="display:none">Todd Anderson and his friends at Welton Academy can hardly believe how different life is since their new English professor, the flamboyant John Keating, has challenged them to "make your lives extraordinary! " Inspired by Keating, the boys resurrect the Dead Poets Society--a secret club where, free from the constraints and expectations of school and parents, they let their passions run wild. As Keating turns the boys on to the great words of Byron, Shelley, and Keats, they discover not only the beauty of language, but the importance of making each moment count. But the Dead Poets pledges soon realize that their newfound freedom can have tragic consequences. Can the club and the individuality it inspires survive the pressure from authorities determined to destroy their dreams?</span>
  <a data-text-id="2684316768460963298" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/41724850-archita-mitra"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1501828497i/41724850._UX100_CR0,24,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/41724850-archita-mitra">Archita Mitra</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Mr. Perry undoubtedly. He wanted the best for his son but couldn't see that his aspirations were suffocating Neil. Neil says he feels "trapped", and h<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Mr. Perry undoubtedly. He wanted the best for his son but couldn't see that his aspirations were suffocating Neil. Neil says he feels "trapped", and his parents instead of trying to help their son try to force their dreams on him. Mr. Keating, on the other hand, encourages Neil to chase his own dreams and confront his demons. He advises him to talk to his father, and compliments him on his performance. His father drags him away. Neil's grades weren't falling. If Mr. Perry had allowed Neil to continue acting on the side as long as he manages his education, he could have saved his son's life. <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17736722924250191597">Dead poets society‬, N.H. Kleinbaum<br/><br/>Dead Poets Society is a 1989 American drama film directed by Peter Weir, written by Tom Schulman, and starring Robin Williams. Set in 1959 at the fictional elite conservative Vermont boarding school Welton Academy, it tells the story of an English teacher who inspires his students through his teaching of poetry. <br/><br/>In the autumn of 1959, shy Todd Anderson begins his senior year of high school at Welton Academy, an all-male, elite prep school. He is assigned one of</span>
  <span id="freeText17736722924250191597" style="display:none">Dead poets society‬, N.H. Kleinbaum<br /><br />Dead Poets Society is a 1989 American drama film directed by Peter Weir, written by Tom Schulman, and starring Robin Williams. Set in 1959 at the fictional elite conservative Vermont boarding school Welton Academy, it tells the story of an English teacher who inspires his students through his teaching of poetry. <br /><br />In the autumn of 1959, shy Todd Anderson begins his senior year of high school at Welton Academy, an all-male, elite prep school. He is assigned one of Welton's most promising students, Neil Perry, as his roommate and is quickly accepted by Neil's friends: Knox Overstreet, Richard Cameron, Steven Meeks, Gerard Pitts, and Charlie Dalton. <br /><br />On the first day of classes, they are surprised by the unorthodox teaching methods of the new English teacher John Keating, a Welton alumnus who encourages his students to "make your lives extraordinary", a sentiment he summarizes with the Latin expression carpe diem. <br /><br />Subsequent lessons include having them take turns standing on his desk to teach the boys how they must look at life in a different way, telling them to rip out the introduction of their poetry books which explains a mathematical formula used for rating poetry, and inviting them to make up their own style of walking in a courtyard to encourage them to be individuals. <br /><br />His methods attract the attention of strict headmaster Gale Nolan. ...<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز دوازدهم ماه ژانویه سال 2002 میلادی <br /><br />عنوان: انجمن شاعران مرده؛ نوشته: ن.هـ کلاینباوم؛ براساس فیلمنامه تام شولمن؛ مترجم: حمید خادمی (لاهیجی)؛ ماهرخ دبیری؛ مشخصات نشر تهران، نشر سمر، 1371، در 190ص، موضوع داستانهای نویسندگان آمریکایی - سده 20م<br /><br />مترجم: زهرا طراوتی؛ نشر نیماژ؛ 1397؛ در 184ص؛<br /><br />روزی رفته بودم دخترم را به دانشگاه تهران برسانم، یادم نمانده چرا در خیابان کارگر رو بالا راه میرفتم، از یک کتابفروش یا دستفروش باز هم یادم نیست، این کتاب را خریدم، و در حال راه رفتن به سوی بلوار، کتاب را ورق زدم، و در حال راه رفتن آن را خواندم؛<br /> <br />فیلم انجمن شاعران مرده، زودتر از انتشار رمان آن، به نمایش درآمد؛ «نیل»، «تاد»، «ناکس»، چارلی، «ریچارد»، «استیون» و «جرارد»، دانش‌آموزان سال اول کالج «ولتن» هستند؛ «نیل» و «تاد»، تحت نظارت شدید خانواده‌ شان هستند، که اصرار دارند پسرانشان پزشک، و وکیل شوند، اما «تاد»، دلش می‌خواهد نویسنده شود؛ در آغاز سال تحصیلی تازه، استاد ادبیات انگلیسی دیگر می‌شود، و فردی به نام «جان کیتینگ»، جایگزین آموزگار پیشین می‌شود؛ روش تدریس «کیتینگ»، با آنچه دانش‌آموزان، پیش‌تر به آن عادت داشتند، کاملاً دیگرگونه است؛ او دیدگاهی نو، نسبت به ادبیات دارد، و از دانش‌آموزانش نیز می‌خواهد، با نگاهی تازه به چیزها نگاه کنند، و...؛<br /><br />نقل از یکی از سکانسهای فیلم: «زندگی را غنیمت بشمار، غنچه های گل سرخ را کنون که میتوانی برچین، زمان سالخورده درگذرست و همین گلی که امروز لبخند میزند، فردا خواهد مرد.»؛ پایان نقل<br /><br />تاریخ بهنگام رسانی 12/06/1399هجری خورشیدی؛ ا. شربیانی</span>

<div id="review_3472105798" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3472105798" />
    <a title="Maria" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/88305529-maria"><img alt="Maria" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598985368p2/88305529.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3472105798?book_show_action=true">Jul 31, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maria" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maria" href="/user/show/88305529-maria">Maria</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3472105798" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4090842118695560152"><b> I went to the woods because I wanted to live deep and suck out all the marrow of life! To put to rout all that was not life. And not, when I came to die, discover that I had not lived. </b> <br/><br/><i> “Carpe Diem,”... “Seize the day. Make your lives extraordinary.”</i><br/><br/>I watched Deat Poets Society movie a few years ago and I still sometimes watch its clips. It’s one of my all-time favorite <br/>movies. A week ago I was scrolling through my Goodreads feeds and found this book and I screamed like <br/>WHAT??? THIS BOOK DO EX</span>
  <span id="freeText4090842118695560152" style="display:none"><b> I went to the woods because I wanted to live deep and suck out all the marrow of life! To put to rout all that was not life. And not, when I came to die, discover that I had not lived. </b> <br /><br /><i> “Carpe Diem,”... “Seize the day. Make your lives extraordinary.”</i><br /><br />I watched Deat Poets Society movie a few years ago and I still sometimes watch its clips. It’s one of my all-time favorite <br />movies. A week ago I was scrolling through my Goodreads feeds and found this book and I screamed like <br />WHAT??? THIS BOOK DO EXIST?!! Boy, the relief I felt.<br />This book isn’t only about poetry, it’s about life, dreams, love, everything. It’s a silver line in the clouds <br />of despair and helplessness we feel. Parents think that they are doing the best with their children but <br />often they don’t. While dreaming big for the children, they don’t pause for a moment to ask them, <br />“what do you want?”, “are you happy” or “what makes you happy?.” The pressure they put on children often leads to what happened at the end of this book. <br />I think every other person who reads or watch Dead Poets Society will feel this connection with the characters because we all have been through what Neil, Todd and the others were feeling. Since I knew what was going to happen, I just couldn’t stop my tears as I was reading Neil getting excited about acting, doing his best to get the part and dancing with joy when he got it. He was so full of life. He brought back the Dead Poet Society, he made his friends do what they love, he was there for Todd, he was just the perfect human being!. And when he took his life, it was like he took a piece of my heart with him. As Todd said, <b>“Neil wouldn’t kill himself! He loved living!”</b> Damn it, he loved living!<br />And Todd, Todd is so precious. His poems, his love, his loneliness, you can’t help but love him and want to comfort him. I love how he stands for the Captain at the end and told his parents off. <br />Charlie, my Nuwanda boy! I love him. And I love Knox. Meeks, Pitts, all of them. My dead poets!!! <br />And how can I forget about John Keating! “O Captain! My Captain!”. Keating is like The Person we all need to meet at least once in our life time and it’s our misfortune that rarely we meet people like him.<br /><br />Here are some of Keating’s quotes:<br /><br /><i>“I say—drivel! One reads poetry because he is a member of the human race, and the human race is filled <br />with passion! Medicine, law, banking—these are necessary to sustain life. But poetry, romance, love, beauty? These are what we stay alive for!<br /><br />“That the powerful play goes on, and you may contribute a verse.” …”What will your verse be?”<br /><br />“And don’t limit poetry to the word. Poetry can be found in music, a photograph, in the way a meal is prepared—anything with the stuff of revelation in it. It can exist in the most everyday things but it must never, never be ordinary. By all means, write about the sky or a girl’s smile, but when you do, let your poetry conjure up salvation day, doomsday, any day. I don’t care, as long as it enlightens us, thrills us and—if it’s inspired—makes us feel a bit immortal.”<br /><br />“Lads, there is a great need in all of us to be accepted, but you must trust what is unique or different about yourself, even if it is odd or unpopular.”</i><br /><br />I can talk and talk about this book. This will ever be in my heart.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4090842118695560152" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer92976904" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer15078642573494231565">I started to read this book -- and couldn't get past the first chapter -- unfortunately, like other, books written after the movie was made, it falls short of literary sparkle. I love the movie though, and for me it will always be a reminder. My husband, son, and daughter were in a stage version of this play -- for opening night. My husband died that night. This is a poem I wrote about the juxtaposition of the two: <br/><br/><br/> In Fifty-five Words 		<br/><br/>Father and son, <br/>Playing father and son: <br/>On stage, the dial</span>
  <span id="freeText15078642573494231565" style="display:none">I started to read this book -- and couldn't get past the first chapter -- unfortunately, like other, books written after the movie was made, it falls short of literary sparkle. I love the movie though, and for me it will always be a reminder. My husband, son, and daughter were in a stage version of this play -- for opening night. My husband died that night. This is a poem I wrote about the juxtaposition of the two: <br /><br /><br /> In Fifty-five Words 		<br /><br />Father and son, <br />Playing father and son: <br />On stage, the dialogue <br />Between the character <br />Neil <br />and his father was electric; <br />Excellent casting decision. <br />It was just like watching them<br />At home.<br />Teenage angst, <br />Overbearing father-- <br />In a way, <br />Disturbing. <br />In the end, <br />on stage, <br />the son committed suicide. <br />At home, that night, <br />My husband <br />Died. <br /><br />PS: It is now nine years later, and while in many ways it has gotten easier, the two will be forever entwined. In remembrance, every year on the anniversary of Kelly's death, I watch Dead Poets Society; it feel fitting somehow. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer7105447468953434778">O Captain! my Captain! <br/>our fearful trip is done, The ship has weather’d every rack, the prize we sought is won, The port is near, the bells I hear, the people all exulting,<br/>While follow eyes the steady keel, the vessel grim and daring;<br/>But O heart!   <span id="freeText7105447468953434778" style="display:none">O Captain! my Captain! <br />our fearful trip is done, The ship has weather’d every rack, the prize we sought is won, The port is near, the bells I hear, the people all exulting,<br />While follow eyes the steady keel, the vessel grim and daring;<br />But O heart! O heart! <br />O the bleeding drops of red,<br />Where on the deck my Captain lies,<br />Fallen cold and dead.<br /><br />O Captain! my Captain!<br />rise up and hear the bells;<br />Rise up—for you the flag is flung—for you the bugle trills,<br />For you bouquets and ribbon’d wreaths—for you the shores a-crowding,<br />For you they call, the swaying mass, their eager faces turning;<br />Here Captain! dear father!<br />This arm beneath your head!<br />It is some dream that on the deck,<br />You’ve fallen cold and dead.<br /><br />My Captain does not answer, his lips are pale and still,<br />My father does not feel my arm, he has no pulse nor will,<br />The ship is anchor’d safe and sound, its voyage closed and done,<br />From fearful trip the victor ship comes in with object won;<br />Exult O shores, and ring O bells!<br />But I with mournful tread,<br />Walk the deck my Captain lies,<br />Fallen cold and dead.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer2161851576495399164">I can't remember ever rating a book at one star. Oh, yes I do. Wuthering Heights. And I HATE that book.<br/><br/>Okay. I love this film. Loved it in the late 80s and I love it still. The movie is not based on the book; the book is based on the movie. <br/><br/>It's not great. It's little more than a copy of the screen play. (Which probably would have been a better read.) The writing was poor. Drab. Telling. The author quoted the school dog. "Gggrrr." I'm not kidding.   <span id="freeText2161851576495399164" style="display:none">I can't remember ever rating a book at one star. Oh, yes I do. Wuthering Heights. And I HATE that book.<br /><br />Okay. I love this film. Loved it in the late 80s and I love it still. The movie is not based on the book; the book is based on the movie. <br /><br />It's not great. It's little more than a copy of the screen play. (Which probably would have been a better read.) The writing was poor. Drab. Telling. The author quoted the school dog. "Gggrrr." I'm not kidding. There were some small liberties taken, as I assume there would need to be for legal purposes. And while they were still poorly written, at least those scenes did not have quite the same feel as having to sit and listen to my mother tell me about a great movie she saw in real-movie time. The rest of the novel did.<br /><br />The plus? Takes less time to read than watching the movie. So I guess if you're pressed for time ... nah. Just watch the movie. It's a bazillion times better. And then you get to watch young, cute, tortured Sean Robert Leonard for 2 hours. It's a win-win.<br /><br />(Now how to be diplomatic about this next week at my first-ever attending of a book club I've joined, for which I read this "novel." I may have to keep on the quiet side.)</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2581634473" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2661844229691924254"><b> 3 / 5 </b><br/><br/>A meaningful book and classic (though I think this was written based off the movie?); it’s a story that everyone should read/watch. That being said, even though I enjoyed this, I was left a little disappointed and wanting more.<br/><br/>While I didn’t see that ending with Neil (but I completely understand why it went that way), I wish the rest of the characters could have been more developed and less <i>dramatic</i>. This entire short novel felt too theatrical and only a few characters seemed to have thei</span>
  <span id="freeText2661844229691924254" style="display:none"><b> 3 / 5 </b><br /><br />A meaningful book and classic (though I think this was written based off the movie?); it’s a story that everyone should read/watch. That being said, even though I enjoyed this, I was left a little disappointed and wanting more.<br /><br />While I didn’t see that ending with Neil (but I completely understand why it went that way), I wish the rest of the characters could have been more developed and less <i>dramatic</i>. This entire short novel felt too theatrical and only a few characters seemed to have their own personalities. The rest morphed together — and for me, characters are (almost) always the most important aspect of the story.<br /><br />I especially didn’t care for Knox and Chris. Quite honestly I thought their so-called relationship was one of the most unimaginable and dull romances I’ve read about. This could be because I didn’t like Knox, and Chris was two-dimensional and moronic (sorry, but not really).<br /><br />For a novel/movie that is so highly praised, I expected to feel moved (or at least more moved than I was). Regardless, I do think this is meaningful and important to book to read, because the message of following your dreams is an important one. But it’s one that could have been done better, with more development and craft.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2661844229691924254" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

And medicine, law, business, engineering, these are noble pursuits and necessary to sustain life. But poetry, beauty, romance, love, these are what we stay alive for.</i><br><br>আহা.. কি চমৎকার একটা কথা!!<br><br>মুভিটা দেখেছিলাম অনেক আগে। নিচের সিনটা রিওয়াইন্ড করে কতবার যে দেখেছি তার কোনো হিসেব নেই!<br><br><blockquote>Oh captain! My Captain!</blockquote><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1507636260i/24133767.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>আমার কাছে মুভির চাইতে বইয়ের আবেদন বরাবরই বেশী। তাই মুভি দেখার পরপরই ঠিক করে ফেলি বইটা পড়ে ফেলতে হবে!<br><br>শুনেছিলাম মুভি থেকে কোনো কোনো বই লেখা হলে সেটার মধ্যে সাহিত্য থাকেনা। কিন্তু এই বইয়ের মধ্যে সাহিত্যের কোনো ঘাঁটতি আমি পাইনি। বরং মুভির মেসেজটা আরও স্ট্রং ভাবে ধরা পড়েছে!<br><br><i>Carpe diem. Seize the day, boys. Make your lives extraordinary.</i><br><br>একেকটা দিন শুধু নিঃশ্বাস নিয়ে বেঁচে থাকাটাই জীবন নয়। প্যাশন কিংবা স্বপ্নে বাঁচাটাই তো জীবন!<br><br>একদল স্কুল পড়ুয়া ছেলেদের এই গূঢ় কথাটা কে বোঝাবে যেখানে স্কুল শেখাচ্ছে ডিসিপ্লিন, অনর, এক্সেলেন্স, ট্র‍্যাডিশন হল জীবনে উন্নতির সোপান! শিখিয়েছেন জন কিটিং, ওয়েল্টন একাডেমীর ইংরেজি শিক্ষক। অতি চমৎকার একজন শিক্ষক তিনি। টোড এন্ডারসনের ভিতরে লুকিয়ে থাকা কবি স্বত্বাকে তিনি বের করে না আনলে টোড কোনোদিন জানতেও পারতো না সে কি চমৎকার লিখতে পারে! মি. কিটিং একজন ট্রু ইন্সপিরেশন!!<br><br>আরও একটা স্ট্রং মেসেজ আছে এই গল্পে।<br><br>বাবা-মা না বুঝেই নিজেদের অতৃপ্ত ইচ্ছেগুলো চাপিয়ে দেয় নিজের সন্তানদের উপর। একবারও জানতে চাচ্ছেনা সন্তান কি চায়, কি তার স্বপ্ন। এভাবে খুন করে ফেলছে নিজের আদরের সন্তানকে! তাই নীল পেরির মৃত্যুর জন্য টোড যখন নীলের বাবাকে দায়ী করে আমি তাকে একটুও দোষ দিতে পারিনা!</span>

<div id="review_1723706027" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1723706027" />
    <a title="Michelle Curie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27203016-michelle-curie"><img alt="Michelle Curie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1600963618p2/27203016.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1723706027?book_show_action=true">Sep 08, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michelle Curie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michelle Curie" href="/user/show/27203016-michelle-curie">Michelle Curie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27203016-michelle-curie?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1723706027" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer640204332297161925"><b>
  <i>"Because we're only going to a experience a limited number of springs, summers, and falls. One day, as hard as it is to believe, each and every one of us is going to stop breathing, turn cold, and die!"</i>
</b><br/><br/>We all have teachers that shaped us more than others. And those who have left a lasting impact on us are to be appreciated and be thankful for. <i>Dead Poets Society</i> is a love letter to an exemplary one and while the message is sweet, the execution is nothing but clumsy. <br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1599636846i/30080776._SX540_.png' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/><b>This is one of those rare</b></span>
  <span id="freeText640204332297161925" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>"Because we're only going to a experience a limited number of springs, summers, and falls. One day, as hard as it is to believe, each and every one of us is going to stop breathing, turn cold, and die!"</i>
</b><br><br>We all have teachers that shaped us more than others. And those who have left a lasting impact on us are to be appreciated and be thankful for. <i>Dead Poets Society</i> is a love letter to an exemplary one and while the message is sweet, the execution is nothing but clumsy. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1599636846i/30080776._SX540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>This is one of those rarer cases where the book has been based on the film of the same name.</b> I didn't actually know this until I was half-way through reading it, quite astonished that a novel so awkward could inspire a film that is considered a classic today. <br><br><b>I'm assuming most people reading this will already be familiar with the plot:</b> We follow a group of friends at Welton Academy, an elitist boys school, where the new teacher John Keating stirs up their routines by challenging them live life to their fullest. <br><br><b>The story is sweet, but the writing just isn't.</b> This is very close to its source material, but Kleinbaum doesn't seem to have fully understood how telling a good story works differently on paper than it does on the screen. It's not enough to show glimpses of conversations and transcriptions of interactions assume that the reader will be able to bond with these characters. What feels like a cliché on screen will appear even more so on page, when there aren't any visuals to even the stereotypes out.<br><br>It's not the writers fault, but it doesn't much help that nowadays the phrase <i>Carpe Diem</i> has lost its power to a considerable degree – no longer it is the enlightening phrase that is going to inspire you to explore every corner of life, but has long become a meme of sorts with it gracing cheap mugs and wall tattoos all over the world. <br><br>At core, this isn't horrible, but maybe do just watch the film and leave it at that. There's nothing more to gain here.</span>
  <a data-text-id="640204332297161925" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1024251570" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1024251570" />
    <a title="♆ BookAddict  ✒ La Crimson Femme" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3089655-bookaddict-la-crimson-femme"><img alt="♆ BookAddict  ✒ La Crimson Femme" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1537761015p2/3089655.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1024251570?book_show_action=true">Aug 11, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="♆ BookAddict  ✒ La Crimson Femme" class="user" itemprop="url" name="♆ BookAddict  ✒ La Crimson Femme" href="/user/show/3089655-bookaddict-la-crimson-femme">♆ BookAddict  ✒ La Crimson Femme</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3089655-bookaddict-la-crimson-femme?shelf=novel">novel</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1024251570" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3599087093054375341">This is a coming of age book which resonated with me. I read this when I was thirteen. Whilst it is a book featuring boys in a boarding school, I could still empathize. The pressures they were under, I felt too at that age. One could say, what kind of pressure and isolation could a 13 year possible experience? It is all a matter of perspective and expectations. For an asian child of immigrants, the desire to succeed and do well are very strong. This book, it was a reading assignment from my eigh</span>
  <span id="freeText3599087093054375341" style="display:none">This is a coming of age book which resonated with me. I read this when I was thirteen. Whilst it is a book featuring boys in a boarding school, I could still empathize. The pressures they were under, I felt too at that age. One could say, what kind of pressure and isolation could a 13 year possible experience? It is all a matter of perspective and expectations. For an asian child of immigrants, the desire to succeed and do well are very strong. This book, it was a reading assignment from my eighth grade reading teacher. We had to write a critical analysis of this story. Did I mention I was in the accelerated track in eighth grade? I was already reading at a high school level and writing papers most public high school juniors/seniors could not write. And I was in a public school system. <br /><br />My reading teacher, Mr. R wanted us to think about how to be different. He wanted us to be able to think for ourselves and determine how we can be inspired the same way that the new English Professor, Keating inspires his students. Reviewing the consequences of this freedom, we needed to ascertain if this was right or wrong. Is it even a question of right or wrong? Perhaps we needed to rethink our actions and determine the consequences of our actions. Can we lay the blame at someone else's feet? These were all thought provoking questions that I still remember almost 30 years later. Whilst I may not have liked Mr. R, my reading teacher (and he was kind of creepy when he touched me), he was the teacher who first forced me to think critically. He opened my eyes to being different. He helped me question my suicidal tendencies and most of all, he encouraged me to continue to read and question. For that, I thank him. When this book was turned into a movie, I didn't think it could get any better. I was wrong. This is the only book I've read where the movie made me cry more, even when I knew what would happen. <br /><br />I love this book and the memories it conjures. I highly recommend every youth who feels powerless to read this book and think again. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3599087093054375341" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText3579704422201573242" style="display:none">"Dead Poets Society" is one of my favorite movies of all times. Actually, I like every movie that has Robin Williams in it. That man brought such joy to life even though he wasn't like that in real life. The world miss him greatly. So it wasn't no surprise I adored the book version of this magnificent movie. I like stories about friendships in a young age because I believe those friendships are the strongest if you find the right person. I also like good teachers. I haven't had much chance to meet those teachers in my school life so far, it's interesting at least reading about them. Though all those good professors seem to end badly. That final scene was so emotional. I knew what will happen but it still hurt. I wish the world have more compassion for great people out there, not just shun them because they're different.</span>

<div id="review_511806311" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/511806311" />
    <a title="Sabrina " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6867494-sabrina"><img alt="Sabrina " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1320338990p2/6867494.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/511806311?book_show_action=true">Jan 20, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sabrina " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sabrina " href="/user/show/6867494-sabrina">Sabrina </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6867494-sabrina?shelf=2013">2013</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer511806311" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13379360057512933318">One of my favourite movies ever!<br/><br/>"The human race is filled with passion. And medicine, law, business, engineering, these are noble pursuits and necessary to sustain life. But poetry, beauty, romance, love, these are what we stay alive for. To quote from Whitman, “O me! O life!… of the questions of these recurring; of the endless trains of the faithless… of cities filled with the foolish; what good amid these, O me, O life?” Answer. That you are here – that life exists, and identity; that the powe</span>
  <span id="freeText13379360057512933318" style="display:none">One of my favourite movies ever!<br /><br />"The human race is filled with passion. And medicine, law, business, engineering, these are noble pursuits and necessary to sustain life. But poetry, beauty, romance, love, these are what we stay alive for. To quote from Whitman, “O me! O life!… of the questions of these recurring; of the endless trains of the faithless… of cities filled with the foolish; what good amid these, O me, O life?” Answer. That you are here – that life exists, and identity; that the powerful play goes on and you may contribute a verse. That the powerful play *goes on* and you may contribute a verse. What will your verse be?"<br /><br />Most people don’t even know that the movie was actually based on a book. Let me tell you, it’s more than just “based on” a book…it IS the book. Almost word for word. The dialogue, the scenes, the plot, the sequence, the characters…everything is the same! When does one ever find that in a book-turned-movie? Having seen the movie dozens of times, I know most of the dialogue by heart. Which is probably why I finished the entire book between nothing more than my morning and evening commute. It was almost as if I was reading a detailed screenplay. There were two instances where the plot goes deeper than the movie, but not enough to make a significant difference.<br /><br />This was both good and bad in my opinion. It’s good to know that a movie can indeed be more than just faithful to the book that inspired it. At the same time, one always expects so much more out of a book. I wanted it to last longer and delve deeper into the relationships between the boys as well as their individual feelings with Mr. Keating. Todd Anderson was the only character who I understood more about from reading the book. Everyone else remained identical to what I see on screen. Nevertheless, I still teared up with Neil Perry’s final scene (I won’t spoil it for those of you who haven’t seen the movie…though I can’t imagine who those people are) and at the final “O Captain, My Captain”s.<br /><br />If you want a taste of the movie without getting up to put the DVD in, then read the book. It’s the identical experience. Moving words, lovely characters, and a heartwarming story about how one teacher can make a difference against all odds.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13379360057512933318" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3519462063" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3519462063" />
    <a title="Nora" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/54271040-nora"><img alt="Nora" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1473761743p2/54271040.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3519462063?book_show_action=true">Aug 30, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nora" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nora" href="/user/show/54271040-nora">Nora</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3519462063" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2209078689864046664">From the start I was intrigued. Frankly, I wanted to watch the movie first, but after a change of mind, I sought the book first. In small number of pages, I was touched and inspired. I never see myself as a poetry admirer, yet I synced with every verse. The friendships and solidarity were heart warming. Not to mention, everyone needs Mr. Keating in their lives. Damn I even teared up a bit at the end. Great book. Now let’s watch the movie.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText10431092104295998135" style="display:none">UPDATE ON THE FUCKING MOVIE<br /><br />i LOVED it. it works so so well. and the gay vibes are so much stronger. <br />i wish that the book would’ve been more like the movie in terms of say, atmosphere, tone.<br />the message came across so much better and i am just gonna forget the damn book.<br /><br />(todd and neil are so so precious i love them so much and they love each other very much)<br /><br />—————<br /><br />i am very much aware that this book is based on the movie and that i have not watched the movie (yet) is fatal but that doesn't change that this book could have been so much better<br /><br />i have so many problems with this book i don't even know where to start<br /><br /><b>tw: emotional, physical, abuse (teacher-student/parent-child), violence, suicide (is this a spoiler??)</b><br /><br />i mean, first of all, i did not expect the suicide. what the HELL was that, lol.<br /><br />i am sure (well not "sure" but i can imagine) that this works really well in movie format, but it just doesn't work as a book. or at least the execution was really bad. there are so many things missing that a good book normally entails:<br />examples:<br />- inner conflicts and monologues and some kind of character development: we kind of see an inner conflict with neil, we also kind of have development with todd, but that's it and it is barely there. there is NOTHING.<br />which leads me to my second point <br />- descriptions?? of the surroundings, the setting, the atmosphere: how about we use some sentences that are not direct speech or "he said". this book is literally just an accumulation of conversations and it makes me furious because this is not a script, this is still a book and it could have been well done???<br />and also <br />- girls. where are the girls? girls that are not necessarily viewed as objects or just exist in the context for boys to lust over. <br /><br />if we are being honest the only good thing is the gay subtext. todd and neil are the only ones that deserve any rights (and i guess mr keating). let them have a happy ending (lol) and i will forget the rest.<br /><br />(were my expectations too high or does everyone else really has shitty taste (or do they rate based on the movie?) seriously what has gone wrong here, i do not understand)</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('AMh2OXdVDJdvdOf7l0g7WidcVpQaNOGzAqvBQ37TQrgkNcMVUliYYcx1VJODgmVTUiLZtU48kGH5ZMroST31lg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('l3wz9N5niHLzO0Mn+jsSAMLMIyhImCbBRI6BQOtZvHqzgYbY+2ochFA68E/u8UwJt7KsCRyQVxO/QYrr3LcLVA==')})
  }

Or the \"Phantom of Fine Hall,\" a figu<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText12874510688436238517\" style=\"display:none\">Stories of famously eccentric Princetonians abound—such as that of chemist Hubert Alyea, the model for The Absent-Minded Professor, or Ralph Nader, said to have had his own key to the library as an undergraduate. Or the &quot;Phantom of Fine Hall,&quot; a figure many students had seen shuffling around the corridors of the math and physics building wearing purple sneakers and writing numerology treatises on the blackboards. The Phantom was John Nash, one of the most brilliant mathematicians of his generation, who had spiraled into schizophrenia in the 1950s. His most important work had been in game theory, which by the 1980s was underpinning a large part of economics. When the Nobel Prize committee began debating a prize for game theory, Nash\'s name inevitably came up—only to be dismissed, since the prize clearly could not go to a madman. But in 1994 Nash, in remission from schizophrenia, shared the Nobel Prize in economics for work done some 45 years previously.\n\nEconomist and journalist Sylvia Nasar has written a biography of Nash that looks at all sides of his life. She gives an intelligent, understandable exposition of his mathematical ideas and a picture of schizophrenia that is evocative but decidedly unromantic. Her story of the machinations behind Nash\'s Nobel is fascinating and one of very few such accounts available in print (the CIA could learn a thing or two from the Nobel committees).<\/span>\n  <a Yet no sooner had he landed an entry-level position at a prestigious firm than Gardner found himself caught in a web of incredibly challenging circumstances that left him as part of the city\'s working homeless and with a toddler son. Motivated by the promise he made to himself as a fatherless child to never abandon his own children, the two spent almost a year moving among shelters, &quot;HO-tels,&quot; soup lines, and even sleeping in the public restroom of a subway station.\n\nNever giving in to despair, Gardner made an astonishing transformation from being part of the city\'s invisible poor to being a powerful player in its financial district.\n\nMore than a memoir of Gardner\'s financial success, this is the story of a man who breaks his own family\'s cycle of men abandoning their children.       <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/1207563-dead-poets-society">Quotes from Dead Poets Society</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;So avoid using the word ‘very’ because it’s lazy. A man is not very tired, he is exhausted. Don’t use very sad, use morose. Language was invented for one reason, boys - to woo women - and, in that endeavor, laziness will not do. It also won’t do in your essays.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/376299-so-avoid-using-the-word-very-because-it-s-lazy-a">1113 likes</a>
            </nobr>
